Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ibra' during Khiyar
Dispute on seller's remission during buyer's khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on ibra' during khiyar and explain the basis for each position according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Remission of price depends on an existing debt; khiyar prevents the debt from being established, so ibra' is invalid according to Abu Yusuf.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq examines a subtle issue: can a seller forgive the price while the buyer still has the option to cancel the sale? The author navigates conflicting reports from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and clarifies the underlying principle that ibra' requires a debt in existence, which khiyar negates. The discussion also extends to the validity of a pledge (rahn) on the price, showing a careful interplay between contractual obligations and property rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal context
Ibra' (remission) is a unilateral discharge of a debt. For it to be valid, a debt must exist at the time of remission. In a sale with khiyar, the price is not yet a binding debt because the buyer can cancel.
The contract is concluded but the obligation to pay is suspended until the option expires.
2
State the two positions
Abu Yusuf holds ibra' is invalid because no debt exists. Muhammad holds it is valid because the contract creates the cause (sabab) of the debt.
Both are reported in authoritative Hanafi sources.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each
Abu Yusuf: the price remains the buyer's property (thaman baqi 'ala milk al-mushtari). Muhammad: the contract is a strong cause for debt; ibra' can preemptively discharge a future obligation.
The difference is over the requirement for actual vs. potential debt.
4
Follow the author's response and exception for pledge
The author (Ibn 'Abidin) argues that ibra' is invalid, but a pledge (rahn) on the price can still be valid because pledge does not require an existing debt. This breaks the analogy that would invalidate both.
Pledge can be on a promised debt ('ala al-dayn al-maw'ud bihi).
5
Determine the practical consequence and tarjih
The thamarah: if ibra' is valid, the buyer pays nothing; if invalid, he must pay. The text implies the stronger view is invalidity (Abu Yusuf's position), as the author supports it and notes the ruling in Jami' al-Fusulayn.
The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad's view is also transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : ذكر في جامع الفصولين أيضاً أن الخيار إذا كان للمشتري فأبرأه البائع عن الثمن لم يجز إبراؤه اهـ. وفي التتارخانية : وروي عن محمد جوازه فينبغي أن لا يصح الرهن أيضاً . قلت : الإبراء يعتمد الدين ولا دين له عليه لأن الثمن باقٍ على ملكه والرهن لا يشترط له وجود الدين حقيقة بدليل صحته على الدين الموعود به وقد بيناه فيما كتبناه من حواشي جامع الفصولين، ولكن نقل بعده أن عدم صحة الإبراء قول أبي يوسف. وفي المعراج أن عدم الخفاء فلم يتحصل من كلامه فائدة تأمل. قوله : فينبغي أن لا يصح الرهن أيضاً) تفريع على قوله «لم يجز إبراؤه» وقوله «قلت الخ» جواب عنه .
If you say: It is mentioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn that if the option is for the buyer and the seller remits the price to him, his remission is not valid. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: It is narrated from Muhammad its permissibility, so it follows that the pledge should also not be valid. I say: Remission depends on a debt, and there is no debt upon him because the price remains in his (the buyer's) ownership, and for a pledge, the existence of a debt is not literally a condition, as evidenced by its validity for a promised debt, and we have explained this in our glosses on Jami' al-Fusulayn. But it is transmitted after that that the invalidity of remission is the saying of Abu Yusuf. And in al-Mi'raj that the lack of clarity... so no benefit was derived from his speech. Consider. His statement: 'so it follows that the pledge should also not be valid' is a branch on his statement 'remission is not valid', and his statement 'I say etc.' is a response to it.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage explores the issue of whether the seller can remit (ibra') the price to the buyer when the buyer holds the khiyar (option to cancel). It presents a dispute within the Hanafi school. According to Jami' al-Fusulayn, ibra' is not valid. However, al-Tatarkhaniyya reports a narration from Imam Muhammad that it is permissible. The author then discusses the implication for the validity of a pledge (rahn) of the price: if ibra' is invalid, then the pledge might also be invalid (by analogy). The author's own view ("قلت") is that ibra' depends on the existence of a debt, but here there is no debt because the price is still owned by the buyer (the obligation to pay is not yet fixed due to khiyar). He argues that a pledge does not require the actual existence of a debt, as it can be for a future debt. Then he notes that the invalidity of ibra' is attributed to Abu Yusuf, implying Muhammad and possibly Abu Hanifa allow it. The passage ends with a critical note that the reasoning in al-Mi'raj is unclear. The operative ruling according to many later works is that ibra' is not valid because the price is not yet a debt (it is still the buyer's property), but there is a strong opposing view. The author seems to favor the validity of pledge despite the ibra' dispute, based on the principle that pledge does not require an existing debt.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibrāʾ): Remission or discharge of a debt; the seller forgiving the price owed.
دين (dayn): A debt or financial obligation; in this context, the obligation to pay the price.
خيار (khiyār): Option or right to cancel a contract; here, the buyer's right to rescind the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which Hanafi imam held that the seller's remission (ibra') of the price is invalid when the buyer has khiyar?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) The author of al-Hidayah

Key Takeaway
Ibra' during khiyar is invalid according to Abu Yusuf (mufta bihi) because the price is not yet a debt; however, a pledge on the price remains valid as it does not require an existing debt.

Destruction During Buyer's Khiyar: Liability
Ruling on destruction during buyer's khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a sold item perishes in the buyer's possession during khiyar and distinguish it from the seller's khiyar scenario.

Core Idea
Possession with khiyar transfers risk: buyer pays the price if the item perishes.

Sales often include an option period (khiyar) where the buyer may return the item. But what happens if the item is destroyed while the buyer has it? This lesson examines the Hanafi position that once the buyer takes physical possession, the risk shifts to him—even if the option period hasn't ended. The key is the interplay between possession (qabd) and the nature of khiyar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the precise scenario: the buyer has khiyar, takes possession (qabd), and the item perishes. State the ruling: 'It perishes at his price' meaning the sale is binding and the buyer must pay the agreed price (thaman).
The term 'هلاك' includes any complete destruction making return impossible.
2
Locate the dalil: analogy to inherent defect
The reasoning is that destruction is like a defect that prevents return. Since the buyer took possession, the contract is effectively concluded; destruction is treated as an inherent defect that precludes revocation.
This is an istihsan (juristic preference) based on the implication of possession.
3
Contrast with seller's khiyar
If the seller has the option, destruction does not bind the buyer because the contract is still suspended; the seller bears the risk.
The difference hinges on who holds the option: the buyer's possession with option implies acceptance, while the seller's option indicates ongoing negotiation.
4
Distinguish thaman from qimah
The buyer pays the agreed price (thaman), not the market value (qimah). Thaman is contractual; qimah is a standard measure.
This prevents disputes over valuation if the price differs from market value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبقبضه يهلك بالثمن ) أي إذا كان الخيار للمشتري وقبض المبيع وهلك في يده فإنه يهلك بثمنه بخلاف ما إذا كان الخيار للبائع والفرق أنه إذا دخله عيب يمتنع الرد والهلاك لا مقدمة يعرى عن عيب فيهلك والعقد قد انبرم فيلزمه الثمن بخلاف ما إذا كان للبائع لأن بدخول العيب لا يمتنع الرد حكماً بخيار البائع فيهلك والعقد موقوف. وفي السراج الوهاج والفرق بين الثمن والقيمة أن الثمن ما تراضى عليه المتعاقدان، سواء زاد على القيمة أو نقص والقيمة ما قوم به الشيء يمنزلة المعيار من غير زيادة ولا نقصان والاستهلاك كالهلاك كما سيأتي وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الخيار للمشتري وحده أو لهما وأسقط البائع خياره بأن أجاز البيع ثم هلك في مدته فإن البيع يلزم بالثمن كما في التتارخانية.
His statement: 'And by taking possession, it perishes at the price' means if the option is for the buyer and he takes possession of the sold item and it perishes in his hand, then it perishes at his price (i.e., he must pay the price), unlike when the option is for the seller. The difference is that when a defect enters, return is prevented, and destruction is not free from defect; so it perishes and the contract is concluded, thus the price is due. Unlike when the option is for the seller, because by the entry of a defect, return is not prevented legally due to the seller's option; so it perishes and the contract is suspended. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: The difference between the price (thaman) and the value (qimah) is that the price is what the two contracting parties agree upon, whether it exceeds or falls short of the value; the value is what the thing is valued at, like a standard, without increase or decrease. And consumption is like destruction, as will come. And he made it general, including when the option is for the buyer alone or for both, and the seller drops his option by confirming the sale and then it perishes during the period; then the sale is binding at the price, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that if the buyer has the khiyar (option) and takes physical possession (qabd) of the sold item, and it perishes in his hands (e.g., the animal dies, the goods are destroyed), he is still liable to pay the full price. This is because the buyer's possession gives him benefit, and destruction is treated like an inherent defect that would prevent return anyway—once the buyer takes possession, the contract is deemed concluded and the risk passes to him. In contrast, if the seller has the option, and the item perishes in the buyer's hands, the buyer is not liable for the price because the contract is still suspended and the risk remains with the seller. The passage also clarifies the distinction between 'price' (thaman: the agreed amount) and 'value' (qimah: the market value). The buyer must pay the agreed price, not the market value, even if it differs. This applies generally: whether the option is solely for the buyer, joint, or if the seller initially had option but then confirmed the sale before the destruction. The ruling is sourced from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Tatarkhaniyya.

Key Terms
هلاك (halāk): Destruction or perishing of the sold item, making it impossible to return.
قبض (qabḍ): Physical taking possession of the sold item, which transfers risk in khiyar cases.
ثمن (thaman): The agreed-upon price in the sale contract, as opposed to value (qimah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the buyer has khiyar and takes possession of the sold item, and it perishes, what is the buyer required to pay?
A) The market value (qimah) at the time of destruction.
B) The agreed price (thaman).
C) Nothing, because the sale was not finalized.
D) Half the price due to loss.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's possession with khiyar transfers risk: if the item perishes, the sale is binding and the buyer must pay the contractual price (thaman).

Effect of Defect Occurring During Khiyar
Rulings on defect during khiyar period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a defect arising in the sold item during the khiyar period, distinguish between permanent and temporary defects, and identify when the sale becomes binding or when the option remains.

Core Idea
A permanent defect during khiyar binds the sale; a temporary one preserves the option.

When a sold item is in the buyer's possession under khiyar (option), what happens if it becomes defective? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the nature of the defect. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling, covering defects caused by the buyer, a third party, or an act of God, as well as increments and seller-caused defects. Understanding these nuances is crucial for determining when the contract becomes final.

Guided Path
1
Identify the khiyar holder and defect cause
Determine who holds the option (here the buyer) and who or what caused the defect: buyer, seller, third party, or natural calamity.
The cause affects the ruling, especially if seller-caused.
2
Classify the defect as permanent or temporary
A permanent defect (e.g., amputation, death) cannot be removed; a temporary defect (e.g., illness, fever) may heal.
The text gives amputation as permanent and illness as temporary.
3
Apply the default ruling
Permanent defect → sale binding, option falls. Temporary defect → option remains; if defect disappears within the period, buyer can still rescind; if period ends with defect, sale binds.
This is the core rule from al-Nihaya.
4
Check for increments (zawa'id)
Increments attached or generated from the item (offspring, fatness, dyeing, rent) prevent rescission. Separate non-generated increments (e.g., profit from resale) do not prevent, but must be returned if rescinding.
Referenced from al-Tatarkhaniyya.
5
Note the exception for seller-caused defect
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if the seller causes the defect while the item is with the buyer, the sale is binding but the buyer can claim compensation (arsh). Abu Hanifa may hold differently (buyer can rescind).
The text cites al-Binaya for this nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كتعيبه) يعني إذا تعيب في يد المشتري والخيار له فإنه يلزمه الثمن لأنه صار بذلك ببعضه، فلو رده لتفرقت الصفقة على البائع قبل الإتمام وهو لا يجوز فلزم البيع وسقط الخيار. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا عيبه المشتري أو أجنبي أو تعيب بآفة سماوية أو بفعل المبيع كما في النهاية ولكن ليس باقياً على إطلاقه، وإنما المراد به عيب يلزم ولا يرتفع كما إذا قطعت يده. وأما ما يجوز ارتفاعه كالمرض فهو على خياره إن زال المرض في الأيام الثلاثة، وأما إذا مضت والعيب قائم لزم البيع لتعذر الرد كما في النهاية أيضاً. وفي الصحاح : عاب المتاع أي صار ذا عيب، وعيبه نسبه إلى العيب وعيبه أيضاً إذا جعله ذا عيب وتعيب مثله اهـ. وقد ذكر المصنف حكم هلاكه في يد المشتري ونقصانه ولم يذكر حكم زيادته عنده. وحاصله أن الزيادة منفصلة كانت أو متصلة سواء كانت متولدة من الأصل كالولد والسمن والجمال والبرء من المرض وذهاب البياض من العين أو لا كالصبغ والعقر والكسب والبناء ورش الأرض، يمنع الفسخ إلا في المنفصلة الغير المتولدة فإنها لا تمنع كما في التتارخانية. وفي البناية أن التعيب إذا كان بفعل البائع في يد المشتري لم يسقط خيار المشتري، فإن أجاز البيع ضمن به البائع النقصان .اهـ. فيستثنى من إطلاق المصنف :مسألتان : ما إذا كان العيب يرتفع، قوله : (وفي التتارخانية كذا في نسخة المؤلف . قوله : (وأما عندهما إذا تعيب بفعل البائع يلزم البيع) أي ويرجع المشتري بالأرش على البائع كما يأتي في شرح قوله «وتم العقد».
His statement: '(such as its defect)' means if it becomes defective in the buyer's hand and the option is his, then the price becomes binding on him because he thereby owns part of it. If he were to return it, the transaction would be split against the seller before completion, which is not permissible, so the sale is binding and the option falls. He made it general, encompassing if the buyer causes the defect, or a third party, or a natural calamity, or the act of the sold item itself, as in al-Nihaya, but it is not absolute; only a defect that is permanent and cannot be removed (like cutting off a hand) is intended. As for a defect that can be removed (like illness), the option remains; if the illness goes away within the three days, the option continues; if the period passes with the defect persisting, the sale is binding because return is not possible, as in al-Nihaya. In al-Sihah: 'āba al-matā' means it became defective; 'ayyabahu' means he attributed defect to it; also 'ayyabahu' if he made it defective; and 'ta'ayyaba' similar. The author mentioned the ruling on its destruction in the buyer's hand and its deficiency, but did not mention the ruling on its increase in his view. The summary is that increase, whether separate or attached, whether generated from the original (like offspring, fatness, beauty, recovery from illness, disappearance of whiteness from the eye) or not (like dyeing, hiring, profit, construction, irrigating land), prevents rescission, except for separate non-generated increase which does not prevent, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. In al-Binaya: if the defect is caused by the seller while in the buyer's hand, it does not nullify the buyer's option; if he confirms the sale, the seller compensates the deficiency. So two exceptions are made from the author's absolute statement: (1) when the defect is removable, (2) his statement: (and in al-Tatarkhaniyya, like in the author's copy. His statement: (and according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], if the defect is by the seller's act, the sale is binding) meaning the buyer gets the compensation (arsh) from the seller, as will come in the explanation of his statement 'and the contract is completed'.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive treatment of what happens if the sold item becomes defective ('ayb) during the khiyar period while in the buyer's possession. The core rule: if the buyer has the option and the item becomes defective (even by his own act, a third party, or a natural event), the sale becomes binding and the buyer must pay the full price. This is because the defect effectively gives the buyer partial ownership (it becomes part of the item), and returning a defective item would undo a transaction that has already been partially executed, which is impermissible. However, this applies only to permanent defects (e.g., cutting off a hand). Temporary defects (e.g., illness) do not immediately invalidate the option; if the defect disappears within the option period, the buyer may still rescind. But if the period expires while the defect persists, the sale is binding. The passage also clarifies that increments (zawa'id) of the sold item during khiyar prevent rescission, except for independent non-generated increments. If the seller causes the defect while the item is with the buyer, the buyer's option remains, and if he confirms the sale, he can claim compensation (arsh) from the seller. This is a nuanced area with exceptions. The text cites multiple Hanafi sources: al-Nihaya, al-Sihah, al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Binaya.

Key Terms
عيب (ʿayb): A defect in the sold item that affects its value or utility.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for a defect; the reduction in value paid to the buyer when the seller caused the defect.
زيادة منفصلة (ziyādah munfaṣilah): An increment that is separate from the sold item, such as fruit picked or rent earned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a slave sold with khiyar to the buyer becomes ill (temporary defect) and then recovers within the three-day option period?
A) The buyer may still rescind the sale.
B) The sale becomes binding because the defect existed.
C) The buyer must pay the price but can claim compensation.
D) The seller can cancel the sale.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on defects during khiyar hinges on the permanence of the defect and the cause, with increments also affecting the option.

Rulings on Destruction of Sold Item Before and After Delivery
Detailed ruling on when sold item is destroyed, who bears loss, effect on sale, with scenarios and internal disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules on risk of loss for a sold item, distinguish between destruction before and after delivery, and apply the rules to scenarios involving the seller, buyer, third party, or act of God.

Core Idea
Before delivery, the seller bears the risk of loss — the sale is void and the price returns.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the rules of khiyar and destruction of the sold item. The Hanafi school has a comprehensive framework for who bears the loss when the sold item is destroyed before delivery. The key principle is that the item is guaranteed by the price (mamoon bil-thaman), so if it perishes before the buyer takes possession, the sale is void and the price is cancelled. After delivery, the risk shifts to the buyer. However, there are exceptions and details when the destruction is partial or caused by specific parties. This lesson walks through the major scenarios and the intra-Hanafi dispute on the seller-caused defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the location of the sold item
Determine whether the sold item is in the seller's hand (before receipt) or in the buyer's hand (after receipt). The risk allocation depends on this.
If the buyer has not taken physical or constructive possession, the item is considered in the seller's risk.
2
Determine the cause of destruction
Was the destruction caused by an act of God, the seller, the buyer, a third party, or the item itself (e.g., animal kills itself)? Each has a different ruling.
Act of God includes natural disasters; seller's act includes misuse or defect.
3
Apply the risk principle
Before receipt: seller bears risk → sale void, price falls. After receipt: buyer bears risk → price due. Exceptions apply (e.g., seller destroys after unauthorized receipt).
4
Check exceptions and partial destruction
If only part perishes before receipt, the price is reduced proportionally, and the buyer has the option to rescind or accept the remainder with a price reduction. If the deficiency is in description (wasf), the buyer can choose to take at full price or rescind.
Description (wasf) includes unstated attributes like quality of grain.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
According to Muhammad, if the seller causes a defect before delivery, the buyer is not bound and may rescind. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the sale is binding and the buyer can only claim compensation for the defect. The mufta bihi is the view of the Sahibayn.
This dispute arises from different interpretations of the seller's act as a disposition confirming the sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب خيار الشرط ۲۳ من وما إذا كان بفعل البائع ولكن ذكر في فتح القدير أن هذا قول محمد، وأما عندهما إذا تعيب بفعل البائع يلزم البيع، وقد وعدنا بذكر مسائل المبيع إذا هلك في البيع الذي لا خيار فيه أو بخيار فإذا كان في يد البائع بآفة سماوية أو باستهلاك البائع أو كان حيواناً فقتل نفسه يبطل البيع لأنه مضمون بالثمن فيسقط الثمن فلا يكون مضموناً بالقيمة لأنه لا يتوالى على شيء واحد ضمانان، فإن أتلفه المشتري والبيع بات أو بخيار له لزم الثمن، وإن كان للبائع والبيع فاسد لزم المثل في المثلي والقيمة في القيمي، وإن بفعل أجنبي خير المشتري فإن فسخ وعاد إلى ملك البائع ضمن الجاني المثل أو القيمة والمضمون إن من جنس الثمن وفيه فضل لا يطيب وإن من خلافه طاب وإن اختار المشتري أيضاً البيع اتبع الجاني بالمثل أو بالقيمة وحكم الفضل ما ذكرناه في جانب البائع واختياره اتباع الجاني قبض عند الثاني خلافاً لمحمد وأثره فيما إذا توى على الجاني وفيما إذا أخذ من الجاني مكانه شيئاً آخر جاز عند الثاني. وإن هلك بعد القبض فعلى المشتري إلا إذا أتلفه البائع والقبض بلا إذنه والثمن حال غير منقود فالبائع يصير مسترداً ويبطل البيع وسقط الثمن عن المشتري وإن هلك البعض قبل قبضه سقط الثمن قدر النقص، سواء كان نقصان قدر أو وصف وخير المشتري بين الفسخ والإمضاء، وإن بفعل أجنبي فالجواب فيه كما إذا هلك كله وإن بآفة Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling statement from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqaiq, expanding on the rulings regarding the destruction (halk) of the sold item in a sale that may or may not include a khiyar (option). The author systematically covers scenarios based on the cause of destruction—act of God, seller's action, buyer's action, third party, or the item itself (e.g., an animal killing itself)—and the location of the item (before or after receipt by the buyer). The core principle is that before receipt, the seller bears the risk (the item is guaranteed by the price), so destruction typically voids the sale and the price falls. After receipt, the buyer bears the risk. However, there are exceptions and refinements, such as when the seller destroys the item after unauthorized receipt, or when the destruction is partial. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Muhammad, if the seller causes a defect, the buyer is not bound, but according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually 'عندهما' usually refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but here it says 'قول محمد' then 'عندهما', so it's a bit confusing; likely 'عندهما' refers to Abu Hanifa? In Fath al-Qadir, it is clarified that Muhammad is alone in his view, and the other two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually the text says 'هذا قول محمد، وأما عندهما' so عندهما refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The author notes this distinction. The commentary then discusses partial destruction and the definition of 'wasf' (description) as an unstated attribute that is part of the sale. The passage concludes with references to al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyyah for further details. The teacher should emphasize that the buyer's right of detention (habis) is crucial in determining liability after receipt.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The option stipulated by one or both parties in a sale to confirm or rescind within a specified period.
هلاك (halak): Destruction or total loss of the sold item before delivery to the buyer.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession or constructive receipt of the sold item, which transfers risk to the buyer.
مضمون بالثمن (mamoon bil-thaman): Guaranteed by the price; the seller bears the risk of loss before delivery because the item is held as security for the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is void and the price falls.
B) The seller must replace the animal.
C) The buyer must pay half the price.
D) The sale stands and the buyer takes the carcass.

Key Takeaway
Before delivery, the seller bears the risk of accidental loss — the sale is void and the price returns. After delivery, the risk shifts to the buyer.

Buying One's Wife with an Option: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on buying one's wife with an option, and effect of intercourse on the right of return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on buying one's wife with an option, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the conditions for returning her after intercourse.

Core Idea
Marriage continues during the option unless the sale is finalized; return after intercourse is allowed only for non-virgins.

This lesson explores a nuanced case where a man purchases his own wife with a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart). The central question is whether the marriage persists during the option period and whether intercourse affects the right to return the wife if the sale is rescinded. The Hanafi school is divided between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace the reasoning behind the ruling and apply it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage deals with buying one's wife with a khiyar (option) and the effect on marriage and the right to return after intercourse. Determine what exactly is being disputed: the status of marriage during the option and the effect of intercourse on the right of return.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, the marriage continues during the option period because the wife does not enter the husband's ownership (she is already his wife). If the buyer later rescinds, the wife returns to her master and remains married. For return after intercourse, it is allowed only if she is non-virgin and her value is not decreased.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the marriage is immediately dissolved upon the contract because the wife enters the husband's ownership. If the buyer rescinds, she returns without marriage. Intercourse absolutely prevents return in their view.
4
Analyze the conditions for return
Abu Hanifa's ruling is qualified: if the wife is a virgin or intercourse causes a decrease in her value (even if non-virgin), return is prevented. Precursors of intercourse (kissing, touching with desire) are treated like intercourse. The passage also addresses buying other than one's wife (a slave) with an option.
Precursors of intercourse are annexed to intercourse because they lead to it.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon one (mufta bihi), as affirmed by Fath al-Qadir and the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the wife remains married or not upon return.

So if he bought his wife with an option, the marriage remains – meaning with an option for him – and this is branched off from the fact that she does not enter the buyer's ownership, so the marriage is not invalidated before the sale becomes effective. When the option falls, it becomes void due to contradiction. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is dissolved because she enters the husband's ownership; so if the buyer rescinds the sale, she returns to her master without any marriage upon her according to them, while according to him (Abu Hanifa) she remains his wife – thus in Fath al-Qadir. And upon this, if he bought his wife in a void sale and took possession, it invalidates the marriage, then if he rescinds the sale due to invalidity, it does not remove the invalidity of the marriage. If she was a virgin, return is prevented according to him as well, and likewise if he kissed her or touched her or she touched him with desire, and likewise return is prevented if someone other than the husband has intercourse with her while in his hand. He said in al-Jawharah: If she was a virgin, the option falls by consensus because he destroyed a part of her, like cutting her hand. And it will come that the precursors of intercourse are like intercourse, and this requires that kissing and touching a virgin prevent return because intercourse prevents it, so these do as well, and that is the meaning of Miskin's statement. Thus the ruling differs between virgin and non-virgin regarding intercourse and its precursors. And what is reasoned in al-Jawharah does not necessitate it, since in kissing and touching a virgin there is no destruction of a part, but it is said that the precursors are annexed to intercourse because they are its cause, so they stand in its place. So if they prevent return, they prevent; if they do not prevent, they do not prevent. And intercourse by someone other than the husband while in the husband's hand is preventive because it imposes the bridal gift (uqr), which is a separate increase generated from the sold item, and it prevents if it occurs after receipt, hence he restricted it with his saying 'in the husband's hand' – reflect. Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute centered on the ruling when a man buys his own wife with a khiyar al-shart (stipulated option). The author explains that according to Abu Hanifa, the marriage persists during the option period because the wife does not enter the husband's ownership (she is already his wife), so there is no conflict. When the option expires and the sale is confirmed, the marriage becomes void due to inconsistency (a man cannot own his wife). According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the marriage is immediately dissolved upon the contract because she enters the husband's ownership, and if he rescinds, she returns to her master (previous owner) without marriage. The text then addresses the effect of intercourse. Abu Hanifa rules that if the husband has intercourse with his wife during the option period, he may still return her because the intercourse is by virtue of the marriage (which remains valid), not by virtue of ownership. The Sahibayn say he cannot return her in any case. However, this is qualified: if she is a virgin, or if intercourse causes a defect (i.e., loss of virginity), the right of return is lost even according to Abu Hanifa. The passage expands to include the general principle that precursors to intercourse (kissing, touching with desire) are treated like intercourse. For a non-wife purchased with an option, intercourse is not permissible for the buyer according to all scholars, but is permissible for the seller. The author discusses various authorities including al-Ramli, al-Asbijabi, al-Jawharah, and al-Mi'raj. The text concludes with a discussion on proof when both parties claim different facts regarding the state of the sold item. The teacher should highlight the practical difference: whether the marriage continues or not during the option period affects the right of return and the permissibility of intercourse.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): The option to confirm or rescind a sale within a specified period, here applied to the purchase of one's wife.
وطء (wat'): Sexual intercourse; here its effect on the right to return the sold item (wife or slave).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man buys his wife with an option and has intercourse with her, can he still return her?
A) Yes, unconditionally.
B) Yes, but only if she is non-virgin or not decreased.
C) No, never.
D) Only if the seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, buying one's wife with an option does not immediately dissolve the marriage according to Abu Hanifa, and return after intercourse is allowed only for non-virgins or if the wife's value is not decreased.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Buying One's Wife with Khiyar al-Shart: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Explaining the ruling on buying one's wife with a conditional option and the effect on marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on buying one's wife with khiyar al-shart and explain the underlying legal basis for the difference.

Core Idea
Ownership transfer during khiyar al-shart determines whether marriage dissolves.

This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the intersection of sales and marriage. When a man buys his own wife with a conditional option (khiyar al-shart), does the marriage dissolve immediately or remain intact? The answer hinges on a fundamental question: does ownership transfer during the option period? Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ sharply, and the practical consequences are significant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the points of disagreement
State the exact issue: a man buys his own wife (who is a slave) with a khiyar al-shart. Identify the two positions: Abu Hanifa says the marriage continues; the Sahibayn say it dissolves immediately.
2
Analyze the underlying principle
Explain the core difference: whether ownership (milk) transfers during the option period. Abu Hanifa holds that ownership does not transfer until the option is finalized; the Sahibayn hold that the contract transfers ownership immediately.
This is derived from the phrase 'لا يدخل في ملك المشتري' in the text.
3
Trace the practical consequence
Compare outcomes if the buyer later cancels the sale: according to Abu Hanifa, the wife remains his; according to the Sahibayn, she returns to her master without any marriage.
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: continued marriage vs. no marriage.
4
Apply to a corrupt sale (fasid)
Note the extension: if the sale is fasid and the husband takes possession, the marriage becomes corrupt. Cancelling the corrupt sale does not restore the marriage—it remains corrupt.
This shows that the ruling for a valid sale with an option is different from a corrupt sale.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Recognize which position is relied upon. The text presents Abu Hanifa's view as the basis, and it is generally the preferred position in the school.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's: marriage continues during the option period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فلو اشترى زوجته بالخيار بقي النكاح أي بالخيار له وهذا مفرع على أنه لا يدخل في ملك المشتري فلذا لم يبطل النكاح قبل نفاذ البيع، وإذا سقط الخيار بطل للتنافي . وعندهما انفسخ لدخولها في ملك الزوج فإذا فسخ المشتري البيع رجعت إلى مولاها بلا نكاح عليها عندهما، وعنده تستمر زوجته ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وعلى هذا لو اشترى زوجته فاسداً وقبضها يفسد النكاح ثم فسخ البيع للفساد لا يرفع فساد النكاح
His statement: (If he buys his wife with an option, the marriage remains) i.e., the option is for him. This is derived from the fact that it does not enter the buyer's ownership, so the marriage is not invalidated before the sale is finalized. And when the option lapses, it is invalidated due to contradiction. According to them (the two companions), it is dissolved because she enters the husband's ownership. So if the buyer cancels the sale, she returns to her master without marriage according to them, while according to him (Abu Hanifa), she continues as his wife. This is in Fath al-Qadeer. Based on this: if he buys his wife in a corrupt sale and takes possession, the marriage becomes corrupt; then if he cancels the sale due to corruption, it does not remove the corruption of the marriage.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the effect of buying one's wife with a khiyar al-shart option. The key difference is whether ownership of the wife is transferred to the buyer during the option period. According to Abu Hanifa, ownership does not transfer until the option is finalized, so the marriage continues. If the option lapses (i.e., the sale is confirmed), the marriage is dissolved because the wife becomes owned by the husband. But if the buyer cancels the sale, the marriage remains intact. The two companions hold that ownership transfers immediately upon the contract, so the marriage dissolves at once. If the buyer later cancels the sale, the wife returns to her master but without any marriage. Abu Hanifa's view is based on the principle that during the khiyar al-shart, the buyer does not yet own the item, so the impediment to marriage (milk al-yamin) does not arise. The passage also extends this to a corrupt sale, where the corruption of the marriage is not remedied by cancelling the sale.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in the contract allowing one or both parties to rescind within a specified period (up to three days).
ملك (milk): Ownership; the legal right to possess and dispose of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man buys his wife with a conditional option (khiyar al-shart), what is the status of the marriage according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The marriage is dissolved immediately.
B) The marriage continues until the option lapses or is cancelled.
C) The marriage becomes corrupt and must be renewed.
D) The marriage is confirmed only if the sale is finalized.

Key Takeaway
The central issue in buying one's wife with khiyar al-shart is whether ownership transfers during the option, which determines if the marriage continues or dissolves immediately.

Intercourse with Purchased Wife During Option: The Core Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer's intercourse during option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the buyer's intercourse with a purchased wife during khiyar al-shart, distinguish between the rulings for a virgin (bikr) and a non-virgin (thayyib), and identify the practical consequence of the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Intercourse with a purchased wife does not automatically block return; it depends on whether she is a virgin.

This passage addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of the buyer's intercourse with his purchased wife during the option period. The key distinction lies in the cause of the intercourse: is it an act of ownership or an act of marriage? The answer determines whether the right to return is lost. Additionally, the virginity of the wife changes the outcome, as intercourse with a virgin creates a defect that prevents return even according to Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
A man buys his own wife (who is already married to him) with a khiyar al-shart (conditional option). During the option period, he has intercourse with her. The question is whether he retains the right to return her and cancel the sale.
This is not about buying a stranger; it is about purchasing one's own marriage rights (a valid contract in Hanafi law).
2
State the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer may return her (if she is a non-virgin), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that he may not return her under any circumstances. The reason is that Abu Hanifa considers the intercourse to be by virtue of the marriage (which is ongoing), not by virtue of ownership (which is not yet complete). The Sahibayn treat the intercourse as an exercise of ownership, thus confirming the sale.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Apply the virgin/non-virgin distinction
Even Abu Hanifa agrees that if the wife is a virgin (bikr), intercourse prevents return because it damages the subject matter (like cutting a limb). For a non-virgin (thayyib), no new defect is created, so return is allowed.
The text cites al-Isbijabi and Fath al-Qadeer for this restriction.
4
Understand the legal basis (ta'lil)
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: The option allows cancellation; intercourse during the option is permitted only because of the existing marriage (nikah), not because of ownership. Since marriage continues, the buyer is not deemed to have accepted ownership. The Sahibayn argue ownership transfers immediately upon contract in a valid sale, so intercourse is an act of ownership that confirms the sale.
This difference stems from a deeper usul principle: does khiyar al-shart suspend ownership (Abu Hanifa) or transfer it immediately with a conditional right to rescind (Sahibayn)?
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If the buyer has intercourse with a non-virgin wife, according to Abu Hanifa he can still return her and recover the price; according to the Sahibayn he loses the right to return. Also, if he returns her, Abu Hanifa says she returns to her husband as a married woman, while the Sahibayn say the marriage is dissolved.
This second branch shows that the dispute extends to the effect on the marriage itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن وطأها له أن يردها) لأن الوطء بحكم ملك النكاح لبقائه لا بحكم ملك اليمين لعدمه، وعندهما ليس له أن يردها مطلقاً لما قدمناه أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا لم تكن بكراً إذ لو كانت بكراً أو نقصها الوطء امتنع الرد كما ذكره الاسبيجابي وظاهره أنه لو نقصها وهي ثيب فالحكم كذلك، وقد صرح به في فتح القدير. وكذا يتفرع أنه لو ردها فعنده تعود إلى سيدها منكوحة، وعندهما بلا نكاح، وقيد بزوجته لأنه لو اشترى غير زوجته بخيار له فوطئها امتنع الرد مطلقاً أي وإن لم ينقصها وسقط الخيار ؛ كذا في المعراج ولم أر حكم حل وطء الأمة المبيعة بخيار، أما إذا كان الخيار للبائع فينبغي حله له لا للمشتري وإن كان للمشتري ينبغي أن لا يحل لهما. ونقله في المعراج عن الشافعي فقال : وللشافعي في حل وطئها وجهان والثاني لا يجوز وهو نصه، وفي انفساخ نكاحها وجهان والثاني لا ينفسخ وهو ظاهر نصه . أما لو كان المبيع غير امرأته لم يحل للمشتري وطؤها على الأقوال كلها ويحل للبائع على الأقوال كلها، وقال أحمد: لا يحل للبائع اهـ . ثم اعلم أن دواعي الوطء كالوطء فإذا اشترى غير زوجته بالخيار فقبلها بشهوة أو ظاهراً وادعى كل استهلاك الآخر فالقول للبائع وأي برهن قبل وإن برهنا فللمشتري، ثم إن كان للبائع حق الاسترداد للحبس صار به مسترداً وانفسخ البيع وسقط الثمن عن المشتري، وإن لم يكن له حق الحبس فللمشتري أن يضمنه القيمة ولا يبطل البيع بينهما .اهـ. قول المصنف : (فإن وطأها له أن يردها) قال الرملي : إطلاقه يفيد أنه سواء كان قبل القبض أو بعده والعلة جامعة تأمل. وفي شرح منلا مسكين : فإن وطأها له أن يردها عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما هذا إذا كانت ثيباً، وإن كانت بكراً امتنع الرد عنده أيضاً، وكذا إذا قبلها أو مسها أو مسته بشهوة، وكذا يمتنع الرد لو وطئها غر الزوج في يده اهـ. قال في الجوهرة : إن كانت بكراً يسقط الخيار بالإجماع لأنه أتلف جزءاً منها كقطع يدها اهـ. وسيأتي أن دواعي الوطء كالوطء وهو يقتضي أن تقبيل البكر ومسها يمنع الرد لأن وطأها يمنعه فكذا هما وهو معنى كلام مسكين فيفترق الحكم بين الثيب والبكر في الوطء ودواعيه، وما علل به في الجوهرة لا يقتضيه إذ ليس في تقبيل البكر ولمسها تفويت جزء لكن يقال ألحقت الدواعي بالوطء لأنها سببه فأقيمت مقامه، فإذا منع الرد منعت وإذا لم يمنع لا تمنع. ووطء غير الزوج في يد الزوج مانع لوجوب العقر به وهو زيادة منفصلة متولدة من المبيع وهي تمنع إذا وجدت بعد القبض فلذا قيد بقوله «في يد الزوج» تأمل .اهـ. قوله : (فإذا اشترى غير زوجته بالخيار قيد بغير زوجته لأن زوجته لمسها بشهوة أو نظر إلى فرجها بشهوة سقط خياره وحدها انتشار آلته أو زيادتها، وقيل بالقلب وإن لم تنتشر ؛ فإن كان بغير شهوة لم يسقط في الكل. وإن ادعى أنه بغير شهوة فإن كان في الفم لم يقبل قوله وإلا قبل. وإن فعلت الأمة به ذلك وأقر أنه كان بشهوة كان رضا كما في السراج الوهاج ولم يذكر المؤلف مما يظهر فيه ثمرة الاختلاف إلا هذه المسألة .
His statement: (If he has intercourse with her, he may return her) because the intercourse is by virtue of the ownership of marriage due to its continuance, not by virtue of ownership by right hand because it does not exist. According to them (the two companions), he absolutely may not return her, as we mentioned earlier. He (the author) gave it absolutely, but it is restricted to when she is not a virgin; because if she were a virgin or intercourse diminished her, return is prevented, as al-Isbijabi mentioned. It is apparent that if he diminished her and she is a non-virgin, the ruling is the same, and it is explicit in Fath al-Qadeer. Also, it branches out that if he returns her, according to him she returns to her master as married, and according to them without marriage. He restricted it to his wife because if he buys other than his wife with an option and has intercourse with her, return is absolutely prevented, even if he did not diminish her, and the option lapses. This is in al-Mi'raj. I have not seen the ruling on the permissibility of intercourse with a slave-girl sold with an option. If the option is for the seller, it is permissible for him, not for the buyer; and if it is for the buyer, it should not be permissible for either. Al-Mi'raj reports from al-Shafi'i two opinions, the second being impermissible, which is his explicit text. As for the sale of something other than his woman, it is not permissible for the buyer to have intercourse with her on all opinions, and it is permissible for the seller on all opinions; Ahmad said it is not permissible for the seller. Then know that the precursors to intercourse are like intercourse. So if he buys other than his wife with an option and kisses her with desire or openly, and each claims the other consumed it, the word is that of the seller, and whichever proves it, it is accepted; if both prove it, then for the buyer. Then if the seller has the right of retrieval due to lien, he becomes a retriever and the sale is dissolved and the price is dropped from the buyer. If he does not have the right of lien, the buyer may guarantee the value and the sale is not invalidated between them. The statement of the author: (If he has intercourse with her, he may return her). Al-Ramli said: Its absoluteness indicates that it is the same before or after possession, and the cause is comprehensive. In Sharh Mulla Miskin: He may return her according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to them, if she is a non-virgin; if she is a virgin, return is prevented according to him as well. Also if he kisses her or touches her or she touches him with desire, similarly, return is prevented. Also if someone other than the husband has intercourse with her while in the husband's hand, return is prevented. Al-Jawhara said: If she is a virgin, the option is invalidated by consensus because he destroyed a part of her, like cutting her hand. It will come that the precursors of intercourse are like intercourse, which requires that kissing a virgin and touching her prevent return because intercourse prevents it, so these do as well. That is the meaning of Miskin's words. So the ruling differs between a non-virgin and a virgin regarding intercourse and its precursors. What al-Jawhara reasoned does not require it, since kissing and touching do not destroy a part. But it is said that the precursors are attached to intercourse because they are its cause, so they stand in its place. So if they prevent return, they prevent; if not, they do not. And intercourse by someone other than the husband while in the husband's hand prevents because of the obligatory dower, which is a separate increase arising from the sold item, and it prevents if it occurs after possession; hence the restriction 'in the hand of the husband'. His statement: (So if he buys other than his wife with an option, he restricted it to other than his wife because his wife: if he touches her with desire or looks at her private parts with desire, his option drops and [?] the spreading of his organ or its increase; it is said [the option drops] even if it does not spread by the heart. If it is without desire, it does not drop in all cases. If he claims it was without desire, if it was in the mouth, his statement is not accepted; otherwise it is accepted. If the slave-girl does that to him and he admits it was with desire, it is consent as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. And the author did not mention from what appears in it the fruit of the difference except this issue.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage addresses the complex rulings when a buyer has intercourse with the purchased wife during the khiyar al-shart option period. According to Abu Hanifa, if the wife is a non-virgin (thayyib), the buyer's intercourse does not prevent him from returning her (because the intercourse is based on marital rights, not ownership). However, if she is a virgin (bikr), he cannot return her because intercourse diminishes her value and causes a defect. The two companions hold that he cannot return her at all, even if she is a non-virgin, because they consider ownership to have transferred. The text then extends the ruling to precursors of intercourse (kissing, touching with desire, etc.) which are treated like actual intercourse—they prevent return for a virgin but not for a non-virgin. There is also discussion about the difference between buying one's wife and buying another woman (e.g., a slave-girl): if the item is not one's wife, intercourse prevents return absolutely. For a slave-girl, the ruling on permissibility of intercourse depends on whether the option is for the buyer or seller. The passage also addresses scenarios like lien, claims of consumption, and the case of a third party having intercourse with the wife. The overall point is that the difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions on ownership during the option period yields these specific rulings.

Key Terms
دواعي الوطء (dawa'i al-wat'): Precursors to sexual intercourse, such as kissing and touching with desire, which are treated legally like intercourse in this context.
ثيب (thayyib): A non-virgin woman, whether previously married or not.
بكر (bikr): A virgin woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man buys his wife (a thayyib) with a conditional option and then has intercourse with her, what is the ruling on returning her?
A) He cannot return her under any circumstances.
B) He can return her because the intercourse is based on marriage, not ownership.
C) He can return her only if she was a virgin.
D) He must pay her a dower but can still return her.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's intercourse with a purchased wife during the option does not automatically block return; the ruling depends on whether she is a virgin and on the imam's view of the nature of ownership during khiyar.

Precursors to Intercourse During Option: The Rule of Dawa'i al-Wat'
Extension of the rule to precursors of intercourse and additional branches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why kissing and touching with desire are treated like intercourse in the context of khiyar al-shart, and apply this rule to cases involving a virgin/non-virgin wife or a non-wife purchase.

Core Idea
Precursors to intercourse follow the same ruling as intercourse itself regarding the right of return.

The previous lesson focused on actual intercourse. This lesson extends the discussion to 'dawa'i al-wat''—the precursors to intercourse such as kissing and touching with desire. The Hanafi school assimilates these acts to intercourse because they are its cause. The same distinction between virgin and non-virgin applies, and additional branches cover claims of absence of desire and the treatment when the purchased item is not a wife.

Guided Path
1
Define the category of dawa'i al-wat'
Dawa'i al-wat' (precursors) include kissing, touching with desire, and similar acts that lead to intercourse. The Hanafi school treats them like actual intercourse in legal effect on the option.
This is a type of legal assimilation (ilhaq) based on causality.
2
Apply the assimilation to the virgin/non-virgin distinction
Since precursors are like intercourse, they prevent return for a virgin (because they cause a defect) but not for a non-virgin (because no defect is created). This is a direct extension from the previous lesson.
The text mentions that Al-Jawhara reasoned that kissing does not destroy a part, but the commentary points out that the assimilation is by cause, not by damage.
3
Extend to cases where the purchased item is not the wife
If the buyer purchases a slave-girl or any woman other than his wife with an option, any precursor act (kissing, touching) absolutely prevents return and cancels the option, just as intercourse would.
Because there is no existing marriage, any such act is purely an exercise of ownership.
4
Handle claims of absence of desire
If the buyer claims that the touching or kissing was without desire, his word may or may not be accepted depending on the situation. If the act was in the mouth (e.g., kissing on the mouth), his claim is rejected; otherwise it may be accepted. If the slave-girl does the act to him and he admits desire, it is considered consent.
This shows that intention and admission play a role in applying the rule.
5
Consider the case of third-party intercourse
If someone other than the husband has intercourse with the purchased wife while she is in the husband's possession, it prevents return because it imposes a dower obligation (which is an increase arising from the sold item). This is treated separately.
The text restricts this to 'in the hand of the husband' (post-possession).

سقط خياره وحدها انتشار آلته أو زيادتها، وقيل بالقلب وإن لم تنتشر ؛ فإن كان بغير شهوة لم يسقط في الكل. وإن ادعى أنه بغير شهوة فإن كان في الفم لم يقبل قوله وإلا قبل. وإن فعلت الأمة به ذلك وأقر أنه كان His statement: (If he has intercourse with her, he may return her) because the intercourse is by virtue of the ownership of marriage due to its continuance, not by virtue of ownership by right hand because it does not exist. According to them (the two companions), he absolutely may not return her, as we mentioned earlier. He (the author) gave it absolutely, but it is restricted to when she is not a virgin; because if she were a virgin or intercourse diminished her, return is prevented, as al-Isbijabi mentioned. It is apparent that if he diminished her and she is a non-virgin, the ruling is the same, and it is explicit in Fath al-Qadeer. Also, it branches out that if he returns her, according to him she returns to her master as married, and according to them without marriage. He restricted it to his wife because if he buys other than his wife with an option and has intercourse with her, return is absolutely prevented, even if he did not diminish her, and the option lapses. This is in al-Mi'raj. I have not seen the ruling on the permissibility of intercourse with a slave-girl sold with an option. If the option is for the seller, it is permissible for him, not for the buyer; and if it is for the buyer, it should not be permissible for either. Al-Mi'raj reports from al-Shafi'i two opinions, the second being impermissible, which is his explicit text. As for the sale of something other than his woman, it is not permissible for the buyer to have intercourse with her on all opinions, and it is permissible for the seller on all opinions; Ahmad said it is not permissible for the seller. Then know that the precursors to intercourse are like intercourse. So if he buys other than his wife with an option and kisses her with desire or openly, and each claims the other consumed it, the word is that of the seller, and whichever proves it, it is accepted; if both prove it, then for the buyer. Then if the seller has the right of retrieval due to lien, he becomes a retriever and the sale is dissolved and the price is dropped from the buyer. If he does not have the right of lien, the buyer may guarantee the value and the sale is not invalidated between them. The statement of the author: (If he has intercourse with her, he may return her). Al-Ramli said: Its absoluteness indicates that it is the same before or after possession, and the cause is comprehensive. In Sharh Mulla Miskin: He may return her according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to them, if she is a non-virgin; if she is a virgin, return is prevented according to him as well. Also if he kisses her or touches her or she touches him with desire, similarly, return is prevented. Also if someone other than the husband has intercourse with her while in the husband's hand, return is prevented. Al-Jawhara said: If she is a virgin, the option is invalidated by consensus because he destroyed a part of her, like cutting her hand. It will come that the precursors of intercourse are like intercourse, which requires that kissing a virgin and touching her prevent return because intercourse prevents it, so these do as well. That is the meaning of Miskin's words. So the ruling differs between a non-virgin and a virgin regarding intercourse and its precursors. What al-Jawhara reasoned does not require it, since kissing and touching do not destroy a part. But it is said that the precursors are attached to intercourse because they are its cause, so they stand in its place. So if they prevent return, they prevent; if not, they do not. And intercourse by someone other than the husband while in the husband's hand prevents because of the obligatory dower, which is a separate increase arising from the sold item, and it prevents if it occurs after possession; hence the restriction 'in the hand of the husband'. His statement: (So if he buys other than his wife with an option, he restricted it to other than his wife because his wife: if he touches her with desire or looks at her private parts with desire, his option drops and [?] the spreading of his organ or its increase; it is said [the option drops] even if it does not spread by the heart. If it is without desire, it does not drop in all cases. If he claims it was without desire, if it was in the mouth, his statement is not accepted; otherwise it is accepted. If the slave-girl does that to him and he admits it was with desire, it is consent as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. And the author did not mention from what appears in it the fruit of the difference except this issue.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage addresses the complex rulings when a buyer has intercourse with the purchased wife during the khiyar al-shart option period. According to Abu Hanifa, if the wife is a non-virgin (thayyib), the buyer's intercourse does not prevent him from returning her (because the intercourse is based on marital rights, not ownership). However, if she is a virgin (bikr), he cannot return her because intercourse diminishes her value and causes a defect. The two companions hold that he cannot return her at all, even if she is a non-virgin, because they consider ownership to have transferred. The text then extends the ruling to precursors of intercourse (kissing, touching with desire, etc.) which are treated like actual intercourse—they prevent return for a virgin but not for a non-virgin. There is also discussion about the difference between buying one's wife and buying another woman (e.g., a slave-girl): if the item is not one's wife, intercourse prevents return absolutely. For a slave-girl, the ruling on permissibility of intercourse depends on whether the option is for the buyer or seller. The passage also addresses scenarios like lien, claims of consumption, and the case of a third party having intercourse with the wife. The overall point is that the difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions on ownership during the option period yields these specific rulings.

Key Terms
دواعي الوطء (dawa'i al-wat'): Precursors to sexual intercourse, such as kissing and touching with desire, which are treated legally like intercourse in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man buys his non-virgin wife with an option. During the option, he kisses her with desire. According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on his right to return her?
A) He cannot return her because kissing is like intercourse.
B) He can return her because for a non-virgin, even intercourse does not prevent return, and precursors follow the same rule.
C) He can return her only if the kiss was not on the mouth.
D) He cannot return her because the option is canceled by any act of desire.

Key Takeaway
Precursors to intercourse (kissing, touching with desire) are legally assimilated to intercourse in their effect on the option to return, maintaining the same virgin/non-virgin distinction and barring return for non-wife purchases.

The Sister Issues of Khiyar al-Shart: Applying the Ownership Principle
Listing subsidiary rulings that depend on ownership during khiyar al-shart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ownership transfer during khiyar al-shart to derive rulings on emancipation, istibra', umm walad, deposit liability, slave's discharge, and wine sale.

Core Idea
Ownership during khiyar al-shart is a foundational principle that ripples through multiple fiqh chapters.

This passage from al-Hidayah illustrates how a single theoretical difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn) produces distinct rulings across multiple fiqh chapters. These 'sister issues' demonstrate the practical weight of usul al-fiqh in shaping furu'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Understand that the foundational difference is whether ownership of the sold item transfers to the buyer during the khiyar al-shart period. Abu Hanifa says no; Sahibayn say yes.
This is the key principle that all subsequent cases depend on.
2
Examine each sister case
For each of the seven cases listed, identify the specific scenario and the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and according to Sahibayn.
The cases: emancipation, conditional emancipation, istibra', umm walad, deposit liability, slave's discharge, and wine purchase with conversion.
3
Trace the legal link
Explain how the ownership principle determines the ruling in each case. For example, if ownership does not transfer, then the buyer cannot validly deposit the item.
The reasoning often involves qiyas or istihsan.
4
Note the practical consequence
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: how the differing rulings affect the parties' rights and obligations in real transactions.
For example, in the deposit case, liability shifts from buyer to seller based on the view taken.

Source Text

وذكر في الهداية أن لهذه المسألة أخوات كلها تبتني على وقوع الملك للمشتري بشرط الخيار وعدمه منها : عتق المشتري على المشتري إذا كان قريباً له في مدة الخيار، ولو كان للبائع فمات المشتري فأجاز البائع عتق الابن ولا يرث أباه كما قدمناه عن الخانية. ومنها عتقه إذا كان المشتري حلف إن ملك عبداً فهو حر بخلاف ما إذا قال إن اشتريت لأنه يصير كالمنشئ للعتق بعد الشراء فسقط الخيار ومنها أن حيض المشتراة في المدة لا يجتزأ به من الاستبراء عنده، وعندهما يجتزأ، ولو ردت بحكم الخيار إلى البائع لا يجب الاستبراء عنده، وعندهما يجب إذا ردت بعد القبض ومنها إذا ولدت المشتراة في المدة بالنكاح لا تصير أم ولد له عنده خلافاً لهما، ومحله ما إذا كان قبل القبض، أما بعده فسقط الخيار اتفاقاً وتصير أم ولد للمشتري لأنها تعيبت عنده بالولادة ؛ كذا في النهاية وفي الخانية : إذا ولدت بطل خياره وإن كان الولد ميتاً ولم تنقصها الولادة لا يبطل خياره .اهـ. ثم اعلم أنهم لم يقيدوا بدعوى الولد وقيده بها في إيضاح الإصلاح قال : لأنه ولد والفراش ضعيف اهـ. وهو تقييد لقولهما. ومنها إذا قبض المشتري المبيع بإذن البائع ثم أودعه عند البائع فهلك في يده في تلك المدة هلك من مال البائع لارتفاع القبض بالرد لعدم الملك، وعندهما من لصحة الإيداع باعتبار قيام الملك ، ولو كان الخيار للبائع فسلم المبيع إلى المشتري فأودعه البائع فهلك عنده بطل البيع عند الكل، ولو كان البيع باتاً فقبض المشتري المبيع بإذن البائع أو بغير إذنه ثم أودعه البائع فهلك كان على المشتري اتفاقاً لصحة الإيداع؛ كذا في التتارخانية. ومنها لو كان المشتري عبداً مأذوناً فأبرأه البائع عن الثمن في المدة بقي خياره عنده لأن الرد امتناع التملك والمأذون له يليه وعندهما بطل خياره لأنه لما ملكه كان الرد منه تمليكاً بغير عوض وهو ليس من أهله وهذا يقتضي صحة الإبراء، وقدمنا أنه لا يصح عند أبي يوسف قياساً، ويصح عند محمد استحساناً ونبه عليه هنا في النهاية ومنها إذا اشترى ذمي من ذمي خمراً على أنه بالخيار ثم أسلم بطل الخيار عندهما لأنه ملكها فلا يملك ردها وهو مسلم، عن مال المشتري إن كانت ثيباً لا يسقط خياره بذلك كالوطء، وإن كانت بكراً سقط خياره به كالوطء، وقد أوضحناه فيما تقدم تأمل. وقوله فقبلها بشهوة «الخ ظاهره مطلقاً سواء كان قبل القبض أو بعده، وتعليلهم بأنه دليل الاستبقاء دليل عليه قوله : (ثم أسلم أي المشتري كما صرح به في الفتح، وأما لو أسلم البائع والخيار للمشتري فلا تظهر فيه ثمرة الخلاف أما عندهما وإن ملكها المشتري لكن يملك ردها .
And it is mentioned in al-Hidayah that this issue has sisters, all built upon whether ownership occurs for the buyer with the condition of the option or not. Among them: The emancipation of the purchased slave if he is a relative of the buyer during the option period. If the slave belonged to the seller and the buyer dies, the seller may ratify the emancipation of the son, and he does not inherit from his father, as we preceded from al-Khaniyyah. Also, his emancipation if the buyer swore that if he owns a slave, he is free, unlike if he said 'if I buy' because he becomes like one initiating emancipation after purchase, so the option drops. Also, the menstruation of the purchased slave-girl during the period is not considered for istibra' according to him (Abu Hanifa), while according to them it is considered. If she is returned by virtue of the option to the seller, istibra' is not required according to him, while according to them it is required if she is returned after possession. Also, if the purchased slave-girl gives birth during the period due to marriage, she does not become an umm walad for him according to him, contrary to them. Its place is before possession; after it, the option drops by agreement and she becomes an umm walad for the buyer because she is defective in his view due to childbirth. In al-Nihayah and al-Khaniyyah: If she gives birth, his option is invalidated even if the child is dead and the birth did not diminish her; the option is not invalidated. Then know that they did not restrict it to the claim of the child, but it is restricted in Idah al-Islah. He said: Because it is a child and the marital bed is weak, which is a restriction to their statement. Also, if the buyer takes possession of the sold item with the seller's permission, then deposits it with the seller, and it perishes in his hand during that period, it is from the seller's wealth due to the raising of possession by return due to lack of ownership, while according to them it is from the buyer's wealth due to the validity of deposit considering the existence of ownership. And if the option is for the seller, and he delivers the sold item to the buyer, then the seller deposits it with the buyer, and it perishes in his hand, the sale is invalidated according to all. And if the sale was absolute, and the buyer takes possession with or without the seller's permission, then deposits it with the seller, and it perishes, it is upon the buyer by agreement due to the validity of the deposit. This is in al-Tatarakhaniyyah. Also, if the buyer is a permitted slave, and the seller relieves him of the price during the period, his option remains according to him because return is prevention of ownership and the permitted slave can do that; according to them, his option is invalidated because when he owned it, his return would be a transfer without compensation, and he is not qualified for that. This necessitates the validity of the discharge, and we have mentioned that according to Abu Yusuf it is not valid by qiyas, but valid according to Muhammad by istihsan. Also, if a dhimmi buys wine from a dhimmi with an option and then becomes Muslim, the option is invalidated according to them because he owned it and cannot return it while being Muslim. Regarding the buyer's wealth, if she is a non-virgin, his option does not drop by that, like intercourse; if she is a virgin, his option drops by that, like intercourse. And we have clarified it previously. His statement 'so if he kisses her with desire...' apparently absolute, whether before or after possession. And their reasoning that it is evidence of retention is evidence against it. His statement: (Then he becomes Muslim) i.e., the buyer, as clarified in al-Fath. As for if the seller becomes Muslim while the option is for the buyer, there is no fruit of the difference; according to them, even though the buyer owns it, he still has the right to return it.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage from al-Hidayah lists several subsidiary issues (called 'sisters') that all depend on the foundational dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding whether ownership transfers to the buyer during the khiyar al-shart period. Each issue is presented with Abu Hanifa's view and the view of the two companions. The issues include: (1) Emancipation of a slave relative of the buyer during the option period—if the buyer dies, the seller can ratify the emancipation. (2) A conditional emancipation (e.g., 'if I own a slave, he is free') triggers immediately upon purchase according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the two companions. (3) Istibra' (waiting period for a slave-girl) after menstruation during the option period—Abu Hanifa does not count it, the two companions do. (4) If a purchased slave-girl gives birth during the option period from marriage, she does not become an umm walad according to Abu Hanifa, but does according to the two companions. (5) If the buyer deposits the item with the seller after taking possession, and it perishes, according to Abu Hanifa the loss is on the seller (because possession is not valid), while the two companions say it is on the buyer (valid deposit). (6) If the buyer is a permitted slave and is released from the price, his option remains according to Abu Hanifa but is invalidated according to the two companions. (7) If a dhimmi buys wine with an option and then converts to Islam, the option is invalidated according to the two companions (due to ownership), while according to Abu Hanifa it may remain. The passage concludes with a note that if the seller converts to Islam while the buyer has the option, there is no practical difference. Each of these rulings demonstrates the practical consequences of the theoretical difference over ownership during the option period.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has given birth to her master's child, thereby becoming free upon her master's death.
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period to ensure a slave-girl is not pregnant before being sold or used for intercourse.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The option to rescind a sale within a specified period, stipulated in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) Both equally
D) It is waived

Key Takeaway
The principle of ownership transfer during khiyar al-shart governs multiple subsidiary fiqh rulings, from emancipation to liability.

When Passionate Touch or Look Cancels Khiyar al-Shart
Ruling on passionate touch/look and khiyar al-shart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which touching or looking with passion voids khiyar al-shart and apply the rule on when a claim of no passion is accepted.

Core Idea
Passionate touch or look voids the option; without passion, it does not.

When a buyer stipulates a period to decide whether to confirm or rescind a sale (khiyar al-shart), certain actions by the buyer can be taken as an indication of satisfaction, thereby ending the option. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains exactly which physical contacts with the purchased slave-woman cause the option to fall – and the crucial factor is whether the contact was accompanied by sexual passion (shahwah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions that void the option
The passage lists three actions: touching, looking at the private parts, and (by implication) kissing. Each must be done with passion (shahwah) to have any effect.
The text specifies 'touching her with passion' and 'looking at her private parts with passion'.
2
Understand the measure of passion for the man
For the male buyer, passion is objectively measured by physical arousal – the spreading (erection) or increase of his organ. A minority view adds that passion can be determined by the heart even without physical manifestation.
The author says 'her limit is the spreading of his organ or its increase' – meaning the woman's limit (i.e., the criterion for her) is the man's physical response.
3
Apply the rule when passion is absent
If the act was completely without passion, the option does not fall in any case. The ruling hinges entirely on the presence of shahwah.
4
Evaluate claims of no passion
If the buyer claims the act was without passion, his word is accepted – except when the act was kissing on the mouth. Kissing on the mouth is considered inherently passionate, so his claim is rejected.
The exception is for kissing: 'if it was in the mouth, his statement is not accepted'.
5
Handle the case where the slave-woman initiates
If the female slave touches or kisses the buyer and he admits it was with passion, that admission constitutes consent (rida) and the option falls.
The text cites al-Siraj al-Wahhaj for this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمسها بشهوة أو نظر إلى فرجها بشهوة سقط خياره وحدها انتشار آلته أو زيادتها، وقيل بالقلب وإن لم تنتشر ؛ فإن كان بغير شهوة لم يسقط في الكل. وإن ادعى أنه بغير شهوة فإن كان في الفم لم يقبل قوله وإلا قبل. وإن فعلت الأمة به ذلك وأقر أنه كان بشهوة كان رضا كما في السراج الوهاج
Touching her with passion or looking at her private parts with passion – his option falls; her (the woman's) limit is the spreading of his organ or its increase, and it is said (it is) by the heart even if it does not spread. If it was without passion, it does not fall in all cases. If he claims it was without passion: if it was in the mouth, his statement is not accepted; otherwise it is accepted. And if the slave-woman did that to him and he admitted that it was with passion, it is consent, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on khiyar al-shart (option of condition). The author states that if the buyer touches the purchased slave-woman with passion, or looks at her private parts with passion, his option to rescind the sale falls. The measure of passion is the spreading of the penis or its increase in size. But there is a view that passion is determined by the heart's desire even if no physical manifestation occurs. If the act was without passion, the option does not fall. If the buyer claims it was without passion, his claim is not accepted if the act was kissing on the mouth (since that inherently involves passion); otherwise his claim is accepted. If the slave-woman initiates the act and the buyer admits it was with passion, that constitutes consent (rida) and the option falls. This ruling is based on the principle that such actions demonstrate satisfaction with the purchase, which is inconsistent with retaining the option. The passage does not provide proofs but simply states the ruling.

Key Terms
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire, passion; here the legal criterion for whether an action voids the option.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The right to rescind or confirm a sale within a stipulated period.
انتشار آلته (intishar alatihi): Erection of the male organ; physical sign of passion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-shart, when does touching the purchased slave-woman cancel the option?
A) Any touching, regardless of passion
B) Touching with sexual passion, as indicated by physical arousal
C) Only if the woman touches the buyer
D) Only if the buyer admits passion in court

Key Takeaway
The option of condition (khiyar al-shart) is voided only by actions done with shahwah – physical passion – and claims of no passion are accepted except for mouth-kissing.
